0451228375|5.0|More years ago than I can recall exactly, I read perhaps the most riveting piece of historical non-fiction writing that I've ever come across, Barbara Tuchman's [[ASIN:0345349571 A Distant Mirror:  The Calamitous 14th Century]].<br /><br />Here, in WORLD WITHOUT END, one can revisit many topics of Barbara's book in a fictional milieu. Here, the story is placed somewhere in south-central England, in Kingsbridge, over a span of time from 1327 to 1361 in seven chunks: November 1327 - June 8 to 14, 1337 - June to December, 1337 - June 1338 to May 1339 - March 1346 to December 1348 - January 1349 to January 1351 - March to November 1361.<br /><br />This novel by Ken Follett is the sequel to his marvelous [[ASIN:045123281X The Pillars of the Earth]], which was set in Kingsbridge some 200 years previous and revolved around the building of the city's cathedral.<br /><br />There are a multitude of characters in WORLD WITHOUT END, the principal four being Merthin, Ralph, Caris and Gwenda.<br /><br />Merthin and Ralph are brothers, the sons of a landless knight reduced to a low state by debt. Caris is the daughter of the most prominent wool merchant of Kingsbridge. Gwenda is the daughter of a petty thief.<br /><br />The plot of the book incorporates the range of human behavior from the noble to the most base against a medieval background that includes two of the most momentous events in English history of that century, the Battle of Cr&eacute;cy and the Black Death: love, hatred, revenge, adultery, ambition, greed, bravery, rapine, cowardice, selfishness, selflessness, devotion, treachery, kindness, cruelty, cleverness, and cunning - why, just like any normal day for me at the office.<br /><br />Along the way, we become acquainted with monks, bishops, nuns, craftsmen, outlaws, reeves, earls, a king, lawyers, thieves, merchants, inn keepers, prostitutes, knights, flagellants, laborers, serfs, and herbalists.<br /><br />What makes WORLD WITHOUT END particularly interesting in general, due no doubt to the author's research, is the insight the reader gets into contemporary practices and methods - how to build the underwater foundations of a river bridge, how to weave cloth, how to build an octagonal church spire, how to slow the spread of the plague, how to flay a man alive - as well as 14th century societal relationships - the Church and the laity, merchants' guilds and towns, landed lords and their serfs, landed lords and free tenants, the King and the nobility, monasteries and nunneries, women and men - and the guiding tenets of feudalism, medical practice, the King's law, Church law, and common law.<br /><br />For me personally, the novel is pure joy because of my familiarity with and love for English history and, after more than a dozen visits, the landscape itself. And most especially because of my several stops at Salisbury Cathedral, which the author has said he used, along with Wells Cathedral, as a descriptive model for that at Kingsbridge. Insofar as the author describes the latter, Kingsbridge Cathedral is very much the Salisbury edifice in appearance and footprint. Or maybe it's just wishful thinking since Salisbury has a double-transept and Kingsbridge doesn't.) B0052UYJDC|3.0|The book contains a lot of practical suggestions for how to reduce clutter, and the author's reminder to ask of every item whether you love or need it is a good one. However, despite the title, I didn't realize just _how_ minimalist Jay's approach is. She assumes that all of her readers share her delight in bare surfaces and near-empty rooms. My working library contains hundreds of books, and not only would most of them be almost impossible to replace, but being surrounded by them also gives me a sense of comfort and abundance. I know other people who get the same feeling from their makeup palette, or shoe collection, or full pantry. Jay's only response to the emotional comfort many of us get from our stuff is to tell us to stop being so attached and lecture us about how we are destroying the planet and exploiting the downtrodden. Between the author's radical minimalism and a hoarder's extreme fear of deprivation lies a happy medium in which, I suspect, most of us would prefer to live - we don't want stacks of paperwork or junk everywhere, but we do want enough tableware to entertain guests, enough craft supplies for hobbies we really do expect to get back to when our current project is finished, etc. For me, Julie Morgenstern's \\"Organizing from the Inside Out\\" was a better fit for the kind of less-extreme decluttering that I was looking for.

1451648537|5.0|THIS is what a biography should look like! I've never read any of Walter Isaacson's other works, but I was just blown away by this one. 1451648537|5.0|[[ASIN:1451648537 Steve Jobs]]  Isaacson's biography of Jobs is fascinating, not just as an insightful look into the life of someone who will likely go down in history as an American business icon, but also because Jobs' commissioning of the book resulted in an unvarnished recounting of his life story that revealed lessons even more important than his impressive accomplishments in the technology field.  Jobs was a genius at his work, in part because he was doing what he truly loved to do. While he did become wealthy as a result, his motivating force wasn't money, but rather the desire to create a company that would endure and produce products that he believed in. That mindset was inspired by the unflagging love and support of his adoptive parents, who made every effort to counteract any sense of abandonment that he may have felt by assuring him that he had been chosen specifically and was loved unconditionally. They encouraged him to follow his dreams wherever they might lead him. The result was a man who often seemed to function in what Isaacson characterized as a \\"reality distortion field\\". He simply didn't accept the notion that certain things weren't possible, and in so believing managed to get others to do what they had previously deemed \\"impossible\\". While it made him difficult to work with at times, he effectively empowered those around him so that they too came to believe in the absence of limitations. That was a profound gift, and was likely the reason so many people were able to see beyond his difficult demeanor and appreciate the relationships they had with him.  He was the epitome of control, focus and intensity when it came to his work, a man with an uncanny intuition that enabled him to know what people wanted and how to deliver it in a form that delighted them. He managed to be perceptive enough to see the big picture, while at the same time maintaining focus on the details. However, when taken to extremes in a manner that was often way out of balance, these same attributes became the roots of his periodic setbacks. Often those with tremendous ability in one area show equally severe difficulties in other areas. Human relationships weren't Jobs forte, and he was excessively judgmental to the point where other people were often seen as either geniuses or idiots. Throughout the course of the book it becomes evident how these character traits permeated every facet of his life. He was definitely a man full of interesting idiosyncrasies. In some ways Jobs may always remain somewhat of an enigma, but this book goes a long way toward trying to explain his actions and motivations.  When faced with his own mortality, it spurred the already prolific Jobs to expedite the release of both the iPhone and the iPad. However, another decision it provoked may well be one of his greatest personal accomplishments. He chose to be interviewed extensively for this biography, and authorized the interviewing of numerous others who played pivotal roles in his life. During his last meetings with the author, Jobs admitted to Isaacson that he \\"had a lot of trepidation\\" about the project. When asked why he'd gone along with it despite his worry, Jobs explained that \\"I wanted my kids to know me. I wasn't always there for them, and I want them to know why and to understand what I did.\\" He also wanted what was said about him posthumously to be accurate, so he wanted his biographer to hear what he had to say firsthand. He didn't insist on seeing what was being written or what conclusions Isaacson had drawn from his many interviews, and he wasn't bothered by the fact that there would be a lot in the book that he wouldn't like.  For a man who at times seemed to have an almost obsessive need to control, a trait likely rooted in perfectionism, this ultimate voluntary surrender of control speaks volumes. It was a tacit admission that he realized his intense focus on the pursuit of the work he loved had yielded success at a significant cost in terms ofhis relationships. You often hear it said that no one on their death bed ever wishes they'd spent more time at work. Human relationships and quality time spent with the people you love are what really matter most when it's all said and done. If you recognize that truth early on, or others who learn it are willing to share their stories, that knowledge can change your life.  I suspect Jobs recognized that there were important lessons to be learned from his life story. Quite possibly he didn't want to be put on a pedestal and revered as the icon the world was making him out to be, but rather in the end preferred to be seen as a complex man from whom as much could be learned from his mistakes as from his triumphs. Jobs' business accomplishments were incomparable, yet here is another message the reader can take away from his story that is perhaps an even more valuable legacy. He did well in courageously choosing Isaacson as his biographer. The balanced presentation of his life that comes across in the pages of this book isn't always complimentary, but it is unquestionably honest, thought provoking, and well documented. I came away with a greater admiration for Jobs after having read it. I highly recommend this book. Enjoy.

Hugh Quarry is a salesman with a major investment firm.  When they meet at a party - both bored - they start talking finance.  Their conversation leads to starting their own hedge fund based solely on program trading, using Alex's specifically designed AI program.  His program is built around the financial markets' Volatility Index (VIX) plus an analysis of bad news - which Alex calls the \\"fear factor.\\"  His program is designed to monitor both the VIX for every financial transaction plus the fear factor at each moment, and then act via direct market trading with no human input.  The program also analyzes its results and adjusts its assumptions based on these results - again, with no human input.  It continues to trade, analyze, and revise its strategy and becomes quite literally unstoppable.  This is an interesting book.  I read it in a matter of hours.  It is well thought out; the program trading AI concept is plausible - not much different than the program trading occurring every day on major financial markets.  The addition of the fear factor is also logical - and reflective of the markets' reactions to the recent debt crisis here in the US and the sovereign debt crisis overseas.  This novel suffers on two counts:  1), I had no empathetic correction with Alex, the main protagonist.  I really didn't care what happened to him.  2), there were too many coincidences used to make the story work.

B0067LY4WG|4.0|I wrote a response to someone else's review of this album basically revealing myself as a former Cohen addict.  I won't recreate all the traveling and collecting I've done over the years but I will say that seeing Old Ideas on the shelf made me feel like I'd seen an old friend resurface after many years of having been parted.  You know that feeling when you try to remember the last time the two of you got together- was it enjoyable?  was it strange?  a little awkward?  was it hard to catch up because it just felt too mechanical?  The last time I 'saw' Leonard was Dear Heather, not a record I would recommend to anyone but absolute die hards (and even for them I would issue a warning of forced writing and lifeless delivery on many tracks).  It was like seeing an old friend in rough shape, wanting to say; \\"Is everything alright?  You got a place to stay?\\"  Then came the Live in London 'rebirth' period of Cohen- where PBS helped to reintroduce Cohen to many who either didn't know him, thought he was dead, had forgotten him or had (like myself) given up hope after his last studio album.  The old friend looked better, dressed well, and apparently had a shave, stopped living in his car and taken up yoga.  \\"You look good!  It's nice to see you again! We should get together sometime!\\"  Old Ideas is the old friend returning yet again- a couple of years removed from the yoga classes, a couple of years from empty pizza boxes on the passenger side floor (okay, I'm exaggerating) with his sh** together.  Again we have our creative and clever old friend back with songs about visiting his stage persona (Going Home), predatory acoustic instruments (Banjo) and songs of faith (Show Me the Place and Come Healing).  The best songs on Old Ideas rival Various Positions best offerings in their sense of melody and lyrics.  Yes, there are a couple of too slow, unmelodic pieces (Amen being one of them, Anyhow being the other).  However, mostly this album has melody (Show Me the Place, Come Healing), has atmosphere (The Darkness), has bounce (Banjo), and thankfully has lyrics that, unlike some more recent material, are accessible and beautiful at the same time (\\"Had to go Crazy to love you, You who were never the one, Whom I chased through the souvenir heartache, Her braids and her blouse all undone\\").  In his previous outings (dating back to the Future in my opinion), Cohen was more like an old friend who'd been drinking and happened upon me in a train station babbling nonsense than the wordsmith I remembered from our earlier days together.  The wordsmith has started showing his face again here.  It's nice to see he got out of the subway and came to his senses.  The music on Old Ideas is less synthesizer driven, more basic.  The songs are less meandering and more brief, to the point.  The album as a whole is a more cohesive piece of art, complete with the songwriter's artistic images decorating the jacket and disc itself.  I would absolutely recommend this record; despite the 20% that I find to be a bit of a drag, this is a very good sign from someone who was rumored to be retiring.

B005IGVYUG|4.0|Now, I might be mistaken about this, but back in the mid-1990's I began buying all of Waits' albums several years prior to the release of \\"Mule Variations\\". My relationship with Waits actually occurred at age 16 when, in 1990, I bought the \\"Red, Hot + Blue\\" compilation with his version of Cole Porter's \\"It' Alright With Me\\". My friends and I had a good laugh and said to ourselves, \\"Is this guy for real?\\" At that point in my pop music education I had not heard anything like Waits, and he certainly was not making music that was like anything else coming out at that time. It did not occur to me that he was influenced by Louis Armstrong or Howlin' Wolf (in fact I don't think at 16 I knew who Howlin' Wolf was, and let me just add here that I don't believe any kid at my suburban mid-western high school even knew who Tom Waits was except me).<br /><br />I also remember seeing Tom Waits on David Letterman around 1987/1988 for \\"Frank's Wild Years\\" and thinking how odd.<br /><br />Now, of course, I am fully briefed and experienced with Waits' oeuvre. I own all of his studio albums and in preparation for \\"Bad As Me\\" I listened to all of them back to back during the past two weeks. This, I feel, helped me put \\"Bad As Me\\" into proper perspective.<br /><br />Waits' has to have had one of the most unusual careers in popular music. I mean, his 70's output barely made any popular dent, nor did it sell very well. The 80's was pretty much the same. However, in the 90's, there was a strange turn of events as somehow his music was rediscovered. How that happened is anyone's guess. By the time \\"Mule Variations\\" was released in 1999, it seemed that more people than ever knew his music and sent that album up the charts making it his highest charting album to date. Since that time, each successive release has climbed higher and sold better. What? The last I checked in popular music the older you get the more you become irrelevant. Yet, here is Waits, age 62, with his highest charting album ever, \\"Bad As Me\\", cracking the top 10 for the first time in his life. So, if \\"Bad As Me\\" sucks, how do you explain its success? Waits is far from a sellout or a pop idol, so you can't say he's sold out and that's why it's gone to number 6.<br /><br />All of this leaves me mystified. But I like it. A lot. More 60+ musicians should crack the top 10. To me, Waits is a national treasure like Randy Newman or Warren Zevon or Lindsey Buckingham.<br /><br />But I digress...\\"Bad As Me\\" is exactly what you have been reading--an amalgamation of everything he's done throughout his career. It's like an abridged version of his career all on one disc in 16 songs. Look, \\"Kiss Me\\" is the closest thing here to something you might find on \\"The Heart Of Saturday Night\\" or \\"Small Change\\", and \\"Pay Me\\" could have come right from \\"Frank's Wild Years\\". Or how about \\"Hell Broke Luce\\"? That could have come from \\"Real Gone\\" or \\"Bone Machine\\". \\"Chicago\\" could have been on \\"Rain Dogs\\" or \\"Swordfishtrombones\\". There's something for every fan of Waits here. This is why it has received the high ratings. Waits does Waits, a real good, too. Now, I can see how if you weren't familiar with Waits that you could have the same reaction I had when I was 16, but if you give his music a chance and you get familiar with it, that initial shock wears off. Also, I can see if you are a hardcore fan, how you might have been hoping that he was going to reinvent himself like he does every decade, only to find that \\"Bad As Me\\" is just a compilation of everything Waits does best.<br /><br />Still, \\"Bad As Me\\" has the right mix of songs both serious and humorous, dour and hopeful, fast, slow, bluesy, jazzy, rocky. The greasy \\"Raised Right Men\\" and \\"Satisfied\\" and the lunacy of \\"Bad As Me\\" and \\"Hell Broke Luce\\", the somberness of \\"Talking At The Same Time\\", \\"Face To The Highway\\" and \\"Last Leaf\\" or the the 50's sock hop of \\"Get Lost\\", there's much to appreciate here. The three bonus tracks \\"She Stole The Blush\\", \\"TellMe\\" and \\"After You Die\\" round out the album in a way. It was unnecessary to put them on a separate disc on the deluxe edition. Also, I did not appreciate the book-like packaging of the deluxe edition--now where will I store it? It won't fit next to all my other Waits' cds!<br /><br />The bottom line, after listening to \\"Bad As Me\\" for two weeks straight is that my wife doesn't enjoy Waits (and I don't know any women who do for that matter), and he has come up with a good album that's not his best work but not his weakest either. Four stars solid. Here is how \\"Bad As Me\\" compares to Waits' previous works:<br /><br />1973 Closing Time: Five Stars<br />1974 The Heart Of Saturday Night: Four Stars<br />1975 Nighthawks At The Diner: Four Stars<br />1976 Small Change: Five Stars<br />1977 Foreign Affairs: Four Stars<br />1978 Blue Valentine: Three Stars<br />1980 Heartattack & Vine: Four Stars<br />1983 Swordfishtrombones: Four Stars<br />1985 Rain Dogs: Five Stars<br />1987 Frank's Wild Years: Four Stars<br />1992 Bone Machine: Five Stars<br />1993 The Black Rider: Three Stars<br />1999 Mule Variations: Five Stars<br />2002 Blood Money: Three and a Half Stars<br />2002 Alice: Four Stars<br />2004 Real Gone: Three Stars<br />2006 Orphans: Brawlers, Bawlers & Bastards: Four Stars<br />2011 Bad As Me: Four Starsunnecessary to put them on a separate disc on the deluxe edition. Also, I did not appreciate the book-like packaging of the deluxe edition--now where will I store it? It won't fit next to all my other Waits' cds!  The bottom line, after listening to \\"Bad As Me\\" for two weeks straight is that my wife doesn't enjoy Waits (and I don't know any women who do for that matter), and he has come up with a good album that's not his best work but not his weakest either. B000000H57|4.0|Melody and atmosphere have become more important to Type O Negative than being offensive and hostile in raging songs of hardcore fury and dark, epic heavy metal. The sound of October Rust leans towards an almost pure form of gothic rock, but played with the volume and energy of heavy metal; nearly all traces of their hardcore punk roots are gone, in favor of a lush, atmospherically layered sound combining the slow dirge rhythms of Black Sabbath, the pop-rock vocal harmonies of The Beatles, the gloomy impetus of Sisters of Mercy, the melancholic elegance of The Cure, and ethereal ambience of Lycia.  \\"Wake up, it's Christmas mourn  Those loved have long since gone  The stocking are hung but who cares  preserved for those no longer there  six feet beneath me sleep\\"  Unlike the previous two albums, which presented a variety of song-styles and expressive tones, October Rust is characterized by a unified sense of the romantic, mostly slow songs of sensitive emotion and sensual desire. The album is a connected whole, with each track either blending into the next or harshly and unexpectedly cut off for the immediate introduction of the following song.  These are intelligent and stimulating songs of intimate longing in styles of laid-back atmospheric rock, somber, impassionate post-punk, and catchy keyboard-driven exercises of dramatic emotion, all structured with a more pronounced emphasis on direct expression in expansive composition. Most of these songs are dark, haunting laments expressing a very real sense of vulnerability that has until now been effectively protected behind the shield of anger and self-deprecating humor.  Extended instrumental passages, some of which seem like episodes of experimental improvisation, and a generally gradual course of unfolding events in slow and expansive rhythmic constructions result in lengthy compositions, usually of an unnecessary application of repetition or excessive elaborations on a conceptual theme. In \\"Wolf Moon\\" and \\"Haunted\\"they come to the ominous presentations of torment that made up much of the previous two albums, but even the colossal \\"Red Water\\", a brooding epic of seasonal mourning and futility, demonstrates a level of orchestration and elegance alien to past releases, along with a broadened application of atmospheric mood and deep feeling.  Peter Steele's remarkably abyssal vocal tone is less inflected with contempt, instead a rumbling, quaking voice of groaning lamentation, erotic, submissive, and reaching a near weeping tone when raising his voice to its highest register, and at times even curiously, yet blaringly, detached, before succumbing to an almost theatrical sorrow and agony.  \\"Now we'll make love by fire light  A blaze so high it lights the night  Long fingernails dug in my skin  Yourself so wet invites me in\\"  Guitar is totally fuzzed out in an electric haze of enhanced distortion, never really producing anything in the way of a distinct riff, but more a droning sound of vaguely shaped rhythmic melody. \\"Die with Me\\" and \\"Green Man\\" employ acoustic guitar in a folk-style mode of reflection, while towering, swooning solos drive home the emotional core with melodic eloquence.  The rhythm is an almost constant motion of slow drifting, with few instances of increased tempo toward rock-oriented standard shifts of momentum, save perhaps for the humorous \\"My Girlfriend's Girlfriend\\", the catchiest, simplest, and weakest song on offer. Churning underneath is the somewhat unsettling rumble of a deep bass guitar providing an element of degraded harmonic support, the bottom-end root source providing the low mass of sonic texture to this otherwise beautiful music.  Josh Silver's outrageous keyboards spread a backdrop of gloomy and ethereal sound rich with dramatic effect. An array of various electronic nuances and effects supply ambient texture, filling the vacant spaces with environmental residue to enhance the experience of this music, which is autumnal, nature-themed, and lovelorn in a fit of restless desire. Piano forms delicate melodies, particularly in the gorgeous \\"Love You to Death\\", while organ adds an almost shivering religious aspect to the sound.  The sound is immaculate, massive and electric as is standard for this band, a tremendous production enhancing the presence and experiential magnitude of the music. For this particular demonstration, the sound emphasizes the high-end elements with an extra sheen of radiance on keyboards and lead guitar, though at levels in harmony with the chosen aesthetic of sound presentation.  \\"Autumn in her flaming dress  Of orange, brown, gold fallen leaves  My mistress of the frigid night  I worship, pray to on my knees\\"  October Rust is an epic and intense work that considerably decreases the hostile element of this band's character as expressed on earlier material, more ambitious and affectionate yet still dark and pessimistic, pagan and deeply melancholy in a more mature and grim awareness of existence. Though this music exists primarily for a manifestation of its own aesthetic, resulting in captivating and exquisitely demonstrated music that uses conceptual evolution as an endorsement to its surface presentation, the strong individuality of sound, charismatic expression, and impressive creative vision makes Type O Negative a thoroughly engaging and intriguing act with mastery for disintegrating atmospheric design of sublime trance-inducing quality.

The books come from different parts of the world. It is not just that they are both long and translate well to English; it's that in both, the abuse of women drives the story. Have there any been parallel high profile novels written in English or other languages? 1592537251|5.0|Gabi Campanario has made two landmark contributions to the world of art in founding Urban Sketchers, one of the largest interactive communities of artists in the world, and by authoring The Art of Urban Sketching, which extends the boundaries of Urban Sketchers to include not only those who produce art, but also those who enjoy the unique and creative way sketching portrays the world around us.  The book provides a unique opportunity to travel the world geographically and at the same time explore the often overlooked mystery and beauty which these artists uncover.  It will no doubt inspire many more of us to take part in the joyful enterprise of sketching, sharing with others the things each of us sees in a unique and individual way.  The Art of Urban Sketching, along with Cathy Johnson's Artist's Journal Workshop, will be particularly useful to the traveler who keeps a travel journal.  It will soon become a classic in the world of art, particularly the art of sketching.<br /><br />The book very intentionally includes a wide diversity of styles, materials and methods in order to suggest that everyone can find a place and a way to both enjoy and create this art form. Some of the sketches require as little as a pencil, a piece of scrap paper, and a few minutes of time.  Other sketches are more exotic, complex, intricate, and time-consuming, reflecting the unique skills, experience and interests of each artist.  The book is unique in that it is not a \\"how-to\\" book on sketching, but each sketch does include such \\"how-to\\" information as time required, materials used, the unique challenges of various categories of subjects, tips from the artist, and insights into each artist's background, interests and signature style.<br /><br />There is an added benefit that this book and Urban Sketchers represent. Seeing and sharing the world with artist friends from other cultures and societies is an exercise in grassroots international diplomacy, leading to understandings and mutualappreciation which cannot help but contribute to a better, friendlier and more peaceful world!  This is a book that belongs in the library of all artists and all who appreciate art and the world it portrays.  Don't miss it.

0060523808|5.0|Gerstner polarises opinion with this book as much as he did throughout his IBM leadership.<br />There is no doubt that Gerstner made tough decisions that were hugely unpopular amongst long term IBMers, particularly around the dismantling of the IBM 'welfare' system and large scale redundancies. These changes undoubtedly changed the company's direction dramatically.<br />The book gives a good insight into how Gerstner saw IBM(tm)s strategic positioning and what, in his view, it could and could not do to change successfully. Sufficient focus is given to why he saw things the way he did to give context to the decisions he made. In doing this, the book is a good portrayal of how the strategy changes were developed and executed.<br />Whilst being specific to the challenges of the IT industry during his tenure the book gives good coverage of cultural and organisational changes that are of relevance and interest to managers in any industry.<br />Gerstner is often portrayed as arrogant, probably with good reason, yet this book has enough anecdotes and examples of decisions not panning out the way he planned to show a different side as well.<br />It is an easy read yet gives enough industry, strategy and execution depth and detail to be thought provoking and informative.<br />I would recommend this book as an excellent, educational read, whether you ultimately agree with Gerstner's approach or not.

Barry Diament and Stephen Innocenzi are both listed as mastering the CD in its booklet - most believe that Barry did not do this one, as the sound of the CD is unlike Barry's typical mastering style.<br />The Shout Factory sounds excellent, but not a lot different from the Victory, which remains the gold standard. Possibly the tape recorders at Island Studios were not gentle with tapes.<br />The Victory booklet is nicer than the later Rhino version as the script on the back of the booklet is in pink on the Victory (which is more consistent with the original front cover) and in white on the Rhino. B000SQKZKG|2.0|Victory USA 383484001-2. Discs made by JVC USA.<br />Victor Japan VICP 64227-8. K2HD Discs made by JVC Japan.<br />Shout Factory 82666310539-1. Discs made by Sony USA.<br />Many years ago, I had a copy of the triple vinyl trifold album on UK Kinney/ WEA/Manticore. The Manticore UK labels in general were either a higher resolution print of a brown manticore on a lighter cream background or the lower resolution darker brown Manticore imprint on a darker, yellowish background. This album had the latter label design, with each internal E, L and P letter of the cover housing an album inside a thin dark blue inner sleeve. Although past albums' recording had their faults, this one was the first time the recording quality of an ELP album had dipped below clear into murkier bootleg quality.  However, the performances contained therein were absolutely first rate and showed that ELP (and in particular Keith) really could do a lot of the blinding speed and multi-tracked keyboards of their less simple, later albums actually live and in front of you. I was of the opinion that there had been no overdubs on this album (splicing of different performances - yes) - the sound was so cavernous and dull, that it would have been nearly impossible to actually replicate this in a studio, without the overdub standing out in an extremely obvious, higher quality fashion. There was great trumpeting of the recording quality in an old Circus magazine I had from 1974 previewing the album, citing quadraphonic sound, concert ambience, concert sound mixer acoustic perspective.... yada yada yada. In reality, it sounded like a stereo Trade Mark Of Quality recording - good quality for a bootleg, with a similar \\"I'm holding a microphone up in the front row of the audience\\" ambience.<br />Let's take the Japanese K2HD copy first, as this one has been modelled after the vinyl album set. The cover is the same in miniature - a trifold with the rear concert picture in over-exposed and red tinted detail. The H.R Giger screen projection on the stage screen is clearly visible. The original very dark blue thin paper inner sleeves haven't been reproduced, but as they were only one colour and had no information on them, this is excusable. The album has been put onto 2CDs without any attempt to join up the original vinyl side breaks into a flowing performance. So, the album fades out and in just as the vinyl sides did. This leads me to the suspicion that vinyl eq. tapes had been used for the creation of this version.<br />The Japanese engineers for ELP albums seem to eschew the usage of NoNoise (which IMHO is a positive on their behalf), but insist on digitally compressing these albums (which is a glaring flaw). Apparently this has become worse as the series has been reissued over the years. Original K2 sets are less affected than the later K2HD and SHM sets. At the opening of the album, the hiss fading up and the retained concert ambience makes you think, \\"All right - they got the sound of the vinyl very nicely here\\". However, at any volume above quiet, the recording becomes quite harsh as any mid to loud transients are just squashed flat. It is very sad that an opportunity at vinyl replication like this has been squandered at the sonic starting line.<br />The Victory USA copy sounds completely different from the Victor Japan K2HD. The tapes (were they the same vinyl eq. tapes or the original flat masters - who knows?) were heavily NoNoised by Scott Hull at Masterdisk, New York and the side breaks were digitally joined by forum member Zal Schreiber at Atlantic Studios, to create a superbly flowing concert.  The concert ambience is nearly gone, however, due to the heavy NoNoise usage and the band sounds like it is mostly playing in a vacuum compared with the Japanese Victor. The original Japanese Atlantic CD issue of this album is exactly the same as the later Victory and Rhino. Cheers for the digital compilation, but great pity about the heavy NoNoise usage. There has been noobvious digital compression that I can hear, and transients are quite prominent without any hardness whatsoever. The booklet has the back cover of the album in a much darker, but less lurid and more natural colour spectrum, printing, but the H.R. Giger projection on the stage screen is now all but invisible. Nothing, really, apart from that, has been lost in this issue in the reduction to a booklet.<br />Finally, we have the Shout Factory CD set, remastered by Andy Pearce. This version has the usual Shout Factory reduction in printing quality, but adds a short essay regarding the album that isn't particularly informative. The CD set appears to be based on the same old Scott Hull NoNoised reworking of the original stereo master tapes, except with even more NoNoise on top of the original, rather marked, noise reduction. There has been some elevation in the bass, as well as attenuation in the treble - this gives a slightly more prominent bass guitar and a bit more kick to Carl's bass drums (both of which were needed), but rolls off the cymbals even more severely (which is way too much). Then there are the odd noise reduction artefacts - in particular a strange inconsistency of volume levels when things get quieter, almost like there are only quantum levels of volume available and the recording is jumping between levels constantly. This is not at all audible in the Scott Hull/Zal Schreiber versions.<br />So, we have the option of buying vinyl for the original experience (both auditory and for the original album graphics), or accepting compromises in the sound that shouldn't have been inflicted upon us for the CD issues.ion that I can hear, and transients are quite prominent without any hardness whatsoever. The booklet has the back cover of the album in a much darker, but less lurid and more natural colour spectrum, printing, but the H.R. Giger projection on the stage screen is now all but invisible. Nothing, really, apart from that, has been lost in this issue in the reduction to a booklet.  Finally, we have the Shout Factory CD set, remastered by Andy Pearce. There has been some elevation in the bass, as well as attenuation in the treble - this gives a slightly more prominent bass guitar and a bit more kick to Carl's bass drums (both of which were needed), but rolls off the cymbals even more severely (which is way too much). Then there are the odd noise reduction artefacts - in particular a strange inconsistency of volume levels when things get quieter, almost like there are only quantum levels of volume available and the recording is jumping between levels constantly. B00687XO1G|1.0|First I ask simply, what is the purpose of making this film? What is the lesson? Do characters grow from their experiences? Is there a moral message behind the film? These are not rhetorical questions - I would like to know your opinion.  In the Lincoln Lawyer, for example, we are shown a defense attorney who comes to the realization that his client is a murderer of willful intent. He is given ample reason to want to make the right decision, including the threats made by his client, and the apparent murder of his colleague. He is given a chance to make things right, both with his past client (who was wrongly-accused) and by putting his current client in prison for murder. And he takes that chance through a series of clever actions.  In Anatomy of a Murder, the defense attorney knows immediately that the defendant is guilty of murder with willful intent. Knowing fully that the defendant is guilty, and *before* even accepting the defendant as his client, the defense attorney gives the defendant a variety of resources and defenses to win his case. Why? And when told that he might not even get paid for his work, he still decides to take on the case. In his own words, \\"I think I'll just stay with you, to make damned sure you get off.\\" Why? What is in it for him?  People often make the argument that, \\"he is just doing his job\\", but it was not his job to take on the case, or to help the defendant *before* accepting him as a client, or to do the job even on risk of not being paid, just to \\"make damned sure\\" the murderer is set free.  Why was Jimmy Stewart chosen for this film? He is shown acting deplorably, with no recognition whatsoever that his choices are wrong. What was the purpose of using his lovable character? To persuade through emotional appeal that his actions are good? To show the audience how easily they can be won over by emotion alone?  Some additional points:  * No chance for him to vindicate his past actions is provided by the plot. The conflict between hischaracter and his actions is never resolved, and appears to have been purposely left unresolved, or even unacknowledged, as if to assume that there are no negative emotional consequences to bad actions - i.e., asserting a mind-body dichotomy.  * No examination is given for why he went from being a prosecutor to defense attorney, and how that has impacted him. Any such examination would have presented a contradiction between the lovable character and his choices - a conflict which would have demanded resolution through character development or final vindication.  * We are never clear on why exactly he proceeded with the case. He accepts the case just to \\"make damned sure\\" the murderer was set free, and he makes occasional reference to the legal fees, as if he is only in it for the money. Is this to demonstrate that he is greedy or selfish? If so, then how is it in his self-interest to set loose an extremely jealous, violent murderer, whose wife has made multiple passes at him. At the end of the film he is shown happily choosing to meet up with the murderer and his wife, knowing that the husband had witnessed his wife make passes at him. How is that a wise choice, if he is just being greedy and selfish?  * At the end, the lawyer is presented with clear evidence that the murderer never believed he was acting based on \\"irresistible impulse\\", by using the term jokingly. How does the lawyer react? By laughing it off.  * Even the title makes no sense, as nobody in the film is interested in examining the murder itself. The reason there is apparent ambiguity is simply because nobody in the film is interested in the truth except as it relates to helping or hurting their case. What actually happened doesn't matter to them - all that matters is that people believe what they claim happened.  I see praise for the film's representation of trial law. Now, if the film had only depicted a trial (a la 12 Angry Men), maybe I could agree. But we are given a preface and afterword, and myanalysis has focused primarily on those, as they are the only means by which to judge the lawyer's decisions during the trial.  As I said, before even accepting to represent Manion, Biegler gave him vital legal advice and coaching, while knowing and explicitly stating that Manion was a deliberate murderer and there was no justification for his action. And after the trial is over, Biegler made clear his disinterest in the consequences of his actions, laughing at the bogus insanity plea.  Any law professor, judge, or lawyer who praises this film must necessarily damn himself.  I'll ask my original questions again: what is the purpose of making this film? What is the lesson? Do characters grow from their experiences? Is there a moral message behind the film?

B002BWP49C|3.0|(Spoiler Alert!)  I've read a lot of the reviews here, and I had to have my say, as it seems most reviewers are either die-hard fans or Twilight-loathers.  I'm somewhere in between.  I have read all the Twilight books, and have seen all the movies.  I am a fan, and for a while, I was an avid fan.  Not so much anymore. Possibly because I got over the hype (and my own immaturity), and also because ever since Eclipse I have found the movies increasingly disappointing.  Twilight is like the junk food of literature, and the same can probably be said of the movies.  The story is captivating, even if it gets a little silly.  The first book was probably the best, and there is a certain charm to the first movie - it was moody and atmospheric, and really captured the spirit of the book.  I was a bigger fan of New Moon.  In my opinion it's the best movie of the four films that have been made so far.  Chris Weitz inspired the best performances, and really delved into that sense of melancholy and heartbreak.  Eclipse, to my mind, focused far too much on the tediously boring and badly acted newborns.  And then Breaking Dawn came along, and made Eclipse look like an Oscar nominee.  It was so disappointing I was annoyed.  Breaking Dawn is definitely the weakest movie so far.  It is also perhaps the hardest of all the books to adapt to film, what with the telepathic wolves and vampire babies.  But it was more than that.  It lacked the indie touch that I liked in Twilight.  It felt like it'd been edited too heavily, and in some places, not enough.  The simpering, over-sentimentalized soundtrack made it sound like a soap opera, and I was surprised by how little emphasis was placed on the wedding, and how quickly it was concluded.  Edward and Bella didn't even get to dance together properly, which was definitely a scene that could have been milked for all it was worth.  The wedding was meant to be romantic, not funny.  There was also a certain awkwardness about the honeymoon, and I don't mean in the bedroom.  Some of the scenes were stilted, the acting stiff.  Worst of all were the wolves.  I laughed out loud when Jacob met up with his buddies for a pow wow.  I don't know how else they could have done it, but it was too silly to be taken seriously.  And these are just some of my complaints.  There are too many to note, or we'd be here all day.  The director finally showed some sense and produced a gripping birthing scene, with enough artiness and drama that it actually woke me up and got me interested.  I even thought he handled the whole Jacob/Renesme imprinting thing pretty well.  (Sorry if you haven't read the book or seen the movie yet.)  But on the whole, the movie was unconvincing, and slightly embarrassing. Not even Robert Pattinson's gorgeousness did it for me this time, which is saying something.  Oh, and that's something else that surprised me - somehow they managed to make a lot of rather beautiful people look as pale and unattractive as Bella did once she got pregnant.  What were they thinking?  Even though I've bought all the other movies on DVD, I'm not sure I can be bothered with this one.  I just hope they learn from the failures of Part 1 and improve Part 2.  I've given the movie-makers a lot of leeway with these films because I'm a fan, but as a movie lover, I'm hoping for a lot better.

B007G5K066|4.0|This 2011 Working Title Films production of Le Carre's 1974 novel 'Tinker Tailor Soldier Spy' has attracted positive critical reviews, been generally liked by audiences, received some well-deserved award nominations and reportedly made a healthy box-office profit on its modest US$21 million production cost, so is an unexpected commercial success for the French investor StudioCanal.<br /><br />Director Tomas Alfredson has delivered a serious film for an intelligent audience, but due to the time-constraints of a single 120-minute feature film, the result necessarily compresses the story so much that you need to pay close attention. A complex plot full of intrigue, double-bluff and the slow revelation of characters' hidden motives through real-time action and flashbacks means if you know Le Carre's novel then you'll be better placed to enjoy the film on first time viewing; if you're unfamiliar with the source material, then seeing the film a second time might make for a more satisfying viewing experience as the number of characters and complexity of the plot can be a bit confusing on first pass.<br /><br />All the cast deliver fine performances with Gary Oldman in superb form as the world-weary but calculating and highly intelligent George Smiley, who has been called out of forced retirement to carry out a discrete investigation to uncover a suspected Soviet mole operating at the highest level of `the circus', the inner core of the UK overseas intelligence service MI6. Oldman has become a fine mature actor and proves here that `less is more', dominating some of his scenes by sheer presence, often with sparse or even no dialogue.<br /><br />The 1970s period detail is pretty accurate with clothes, hairstyles, cars, interiors and the drab accoutrements of office life - paper files, tele-printers, manual typewriters, telephones, dreary furniture - setting the tone. There's not much color here, and the film's look is bleak and washed-out to reinforce the subject matter and the mood. The scenes set in Budapest and Istanbul look even bleaker and greyer than London.<br /><br />The film is true in spirit to Le Carre's 1974 novel, so not natural Hollywood fodder. There is no `hero' (Smiley is a kind of anti-hero); all the characters are in some way flawed and none very sympathetic; there is little cinematic action, no gun battles, explosions or car chases. There is precious little humor, but it's not that kind of film. The few scenes of violence are brief and understated, casual and shocking but never dwelled on. The audience is invited to pay attention, to watch the characters closely, to listen to the dialogue and think. The duplicitous and closed world of the secretive cold war spying game is very well realised, and has little in common with the glamorous, fast-paced eye-candy of James Bond films.<br /><br />If you like your entertainment served on a plastic plate with fries and fizzy cola to wash it down, best steer clear of `Tinker Tailor'. This is a film for grown-ups, and a more sophisticated palette will better savour its qualities.<br /><br />See if you can spot John Le Carre himself in a brief cameo rolethe mood.
